<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Soil</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Soil_(disambiguation)" title="Soil (disambiguation)">Soil (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Estructura-suelo.jpg"><img alt="This is a diagram and related photograph of soil layers from bedrock to soil." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="541" data-file-width="513" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Estructura-suelo.jpg/220px-Estructura-suelo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Estructura-suelo.jpg/330px-Estructura-suelo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Estructura-suelo.jpg/440px-Estructura-suelo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Estructura-suelo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A, B, and C represent the soil profile, a notation firstly coined by <a href="/wiki/Vasily_Dokuchaev" title="Vasily Dokuchaev">Vasily Dokuchaev</a>, the father of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pedology_(soil_study)" title="Pedology (soil study)">pedology</a>; A is the <a href="/wiki/Topsoil" title="Topsoil">topsoil</a>; B is a <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">regolith</a>; C is a <a href="/wiki/Saprolite" title="Saprolite">saprolite</a>, a less-weathered regolith; the bottom-most layer represents the <a href="/wiki/Bedrock" title="Bedrock">bedrock</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stagnogley.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1179" data-file-width="1511" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Stagnogley.JPG/220px-Stagnogley.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Stagnogley.JPG/330px-Stagnogley.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Stagnogley.JPG/440px-Stagnogley.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stagnogley.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Surface-water-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gley_soil" title="Gley soil">gley</a> developed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glacial_till" title="Glacial till">glacial till</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Soil</b> is a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Organic_matter" title="Organic matter">organic matter</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minerals" title="Minerals">minerals</a>, gases, liquids, and organisms that together support life. The Earth's body of soil is the <a href="/wiki/Pedosphere" title="Pedosphere">pedosphere</a>, which has four important functions: it is a medium for plant growth; it is a means of <a href="/wiki/Water_storage" title="Water storage">water storage</a>, supply and purification; it is a modifier of <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth's atmosphere</a>; it is a habitat for organisms; all of which, in turn, modify the soil.</p>
<p>Soil has been called the <i>Skin of the Earth</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> as it interfaces with the <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The term <i>pedolith</i>, used commonly to refer to the soil, literally translates <i>ground stone</i>. Soil consists of a solid phase of minerals and organic matter (the soil matrix), as well as a <a href="/wiki/Porosity" title="Porosity">porous</a> phase that holds gases (the soil atmosphere) and water (the soil solution).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Accordingly, soils are often treated as a three-<a href="/wiki/State_of_matter" title="State of matter">state</a> system of solids, liquids, and gases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Soil is a product of the influence of <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">relief</a> (elevation, orientation, and slope of terrain), organisms, and its <a href="/wiki/Parent_material" title="Parent material">parent materials</a> (original minerals) interacting over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilluly1975_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilluly1975-7">[7]</a></sup> Soil continually undergoes development by way of numerous physical, chemical and biological processes, which include <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> with associated <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>. Given its complexity and strong internal <a href="/wiki/Connectedness" title="Connectedness">connectedness</a> soil has been considered as an <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> by <a href="/wiki/Soil_ecology" title="Soil ecology">soil ecologists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most soils have a dry <a href="/wiki/Bulk_density" title="Bulk density">bulk density</a> (density of soil taking into account voids when dry) between 1.1 and 1.6 g/cm<sup>3</sup>, while the soil <a href="/wiki/Particle_density_(packed_density)" title="Particle density (packed density)">particle density</a> is much higher, in the range of 2.6 to 2.7 g/cm<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Little of the soil of planet Earth is older than the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> and none is older than the <a href="/wiki/Cenozoic" title="Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buol_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buol-10">[10]</a></sup> although <a href="/wiki/Paleopedological_record" title="Paleopedological record">fossilized soils</a> are preserved from as far back as the <a href="/wiki/Archean" title="Archean">Archean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Soil_science" title="Soil science">Soil science</a> has two basic branches of study: <a href="/wiki/Edaphology" title="Edaphology">edaphology</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pedology_(soil_study)" title="Pedology (soil study)">pedology</a>. Edaphology is concerned with the influence of soils on living things.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Pedology is focused on the formation, description (morphology), and classification of soils in their natural environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In engineering terms, soil is included in the broader concept of <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">regolith</a>, which also includes other loose material that lies above the <i>solid geology</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Soil is commonly referred to as <i>earth</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Dirt" title="Dirt">dirt</a></i>; technically, the term <i>dirt</i> should be restricted to displaced soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soil_profile.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1833" data-file-width="1500" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Soil_profile.png/220px-Soil_profile.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Soil_profile.png/330px-Soil_profile.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Soil_profile.png/440px-Soil_profile.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Soil_profile.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Soil Profile: Darkened topsoil and reddish subsoil <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soil_horizons" title="Soil horizons">layers</a> are typical in <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">some regions.</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Functions_of_soils">Functions of soils</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Functions of soils">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Soil is a major component of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a>. The world's ecosystems are impacted in far-reaching ways by the processes carried out in the soil, from <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">ozone depletion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainforest_destruction" title="Rainforest destruction">rainforest destruction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>. With respect to Earth's <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>, soil is an important <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">carbon reservoir</a>, and it is potentially one of the most reactive to human disturbance<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> and climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davidson_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson-17">[17]</a></sup> As the planet warms, it has been predicted that soils will add carbon dioxide to the atmosphere due to increased <a href="/wiki/Soil_biology" title="Soil biology">biological</a> activity at higher temperatures, a <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a> (amplification).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> This prediction has, however, been questioned on consideration of more recent knowledge on soil carbon turnover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Soil acts as an engineering medium, a habitat for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soil_organisms" title="Soil organisms">soil organisms</a>, a recycling system for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutrients" title="Nutrients">nutrients</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_waste" title="Organic waste">organic wastes</a>, a regulator of <a href="/wiki/Water_quality" title="Water quality">water quality</a>, a modifier of <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_chemistry" title="Atmospheric chemistry">atmospheric composition</a>, and a medium for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_growth" title="Plant growth">plant growth</a>, making it a critically important provider of <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Since soil has a tremendous range of available niches and habitats, it contains most of the Earth's genetic diversity. A gram of soil can contain billions of organisms, belonging to thousands of species, mostly microbial and in the main still unexplored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Soil has a mean prokaryotic density of roughly 10<sup>8</sup> organisms per gram,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> whereas the ocean has no more than 10<sup>7</sup> procaryotic organisms per milliliter (gram) of seawater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Soil_organic_matter" title="Soil organic matter">Organic carbon</a> held in soil is eventually returned to the atmosphere through the process of <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">respiration</a> carried out by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterotrophic" title="Heterotrophic">heterotrophic</a> organisms, but a substantial part is retained in the soil in the form of <a href="/wiki/Soil_organic_matter" title="Soil organic matter">soil organic matter</a>; <a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">tillage</a> usually increases the rate of respiration, leading to the depletion of soil organic matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Since plant roots need oxygen, ventilation is an important characteristic of soil. This ventilation can be accomplished via networks of interconnected <a href="/wiki/Pore_space_in_soil" title="Pore space in soil">soil pores</a>, which also absorb and hold rainwater making it readily available for plant uptake. Since plants require a nearly continuous supply of water, but most regions receive sporadic rainfall, the <a href="/wiki/Soil_water_(retention)" title="Soil water (retention)">water-holding capacity</a> of soils is vital for plant survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Soils can effectively remove impurities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> kill disease agents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and degrade contaminants, this latter property being called natural attenuation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Typically, soils maintain a net absorption of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, and undergo a net release of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Soils offer plants physical support, air, water, temperature moderation, nutrients, and protection from toxins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Soils provide readily available nutrients to plants and animals by converting dead organic matter into various nutrient forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Soil&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright" style="">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:200px">
<div style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden;border-radius:100px;border:1px solid black">

<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:grey"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 200px 0;border-color:grey"></div>
<div class="transborder" style="position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 82.727194597249px; border-left-color:brown"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:brown"></div>
